        </td>
        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        
        
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Mike Moustakas' not-quite-world-storming 2011 debut is less surprising when you remember that the only time he really blew anyone away was in 298 Double-A plate appearances in 2010. Don't get me wrong, there were and are other reasons to think Moustakas will be good -- mostly his ability to make contact and his power -- but it now seems laughable that he was ever ranked ahead of Eric Hosmer by anyone. Much was made of Moustakas' hot streak to finish 2011, but those sorts of splits are almost always misused. Moustakas does not take many walks and is not fast. He's going to have to "earn his average" by continuing to avoid strikeouts and showing the power he had in the minors. But if you have to pick two skills to have as a hitter, those are pretty good ones to build on. Don't get me wrong -- Moustakas still has plenty of potential, and that potential should lead you to be willing to go out on a limb for him more than you would for an old third baseman of less pedigree projected to hit something like .265/.305/.444 with 20 home runs. Moreover, third base is not exactly laden with monster hitters. Don't be afraid to invest in Moustakas, who is likely to be a good player. But only do so if you are willing to be patient, as there are probably going to be some bumps along the way. (Matt Klaasen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Mike Moustakas did not exactly take Kansas City by storm in 2011. However, for those willing to invest and be patient, he could pay fantasy dividends down the road.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Moustakas' first half of the season (.268/.327/.490 with 15 home runs) was great. In the second half, his production dropped significantly (.211/.261/.325 with five homers). The most likely cause for the drop in production is an accumulation of injuries. He had hamstring problems in May, and knee problems in July and August. Finally, he missed the last few games of the season with a groin injury. Even if he comes back 100% healthy in 2013, he needs to address a couple other issues to maximize his value. First, he needs to get his 18% infield fly rate down. It was the fourth-highest value among qualified hitters in 2012. Also, a lower strikeout value (20% in 2012) would be helpful. His batting average won't be great -- about 40% of his at-bats end in a strikeout or a pop up. Besides the low average, he has good power potential. Don't be surprised to see him hit 30+ HRs. Moose Tacos should not be avoided, just know the risk associated with the low batting average and non-existent speed. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Last season started out as breakout season for Moustakas, but injuries may have derailed him in the end. Expect a rebound in 2013.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Mike Moustakas is trying to be a home run hitter. The problem is is isn't one. About 45% of his hits are fly balls and just over 7% of them have gone for home runs over the past three seasons. A 7% home run per fly ball ratio is similar to values put up by Michael Young, Jimmy Rollins and Carl Crawford. Besides not hitting many home runs, he is fifth in the league (min 1000 plate appearances) in pop-up percentage at 18.1%. An all fly ball, no-homer hitter is bad fantasy combo leading to minimal home runs and low batting average. There isn't a lot of room for improvement, either. His fly ball and homer distances have been 267, 279, 270 feet -- all way below league average. He will still be hitting in Kauffman Stadium, which suppresses home runs. He hit four home runs for two months before the hitting coach change and eight for the four months after the change. Since he has no speed, he really is pretty much useless in fantasy baseball. Right now, I see him at best hitting .250 with 15 home runs, two stolen bases and 120 Runs+RBI. He could always hit worse, too. I would not pay over $1 or a last-round pick for him in any format. Maybe see if he somehow finds his power stroke -- if not, cut bait immediately. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Mike Moustakas' value lies in his ability to hit home runs, which he sucks at. Currently, there are better about 29 better options at his position.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Moustakas had a great spring training and postseason. Those periods surrounded a horrible regular season. We are seeing what Moustakas has become as a hitter, and it is not pretty. His average has declined every year in the majors (.263 to .242 to .233 to .212) mainly because teams are using the shift more and more against him. In 2014, he hit into the shift more than all but eight major league hitters. With his heavy pull tendencies, he should be able to hit a few home runs, but he was managed 15 last season. Besides the lack of elite power, he has no stolen base ability (1 stolen base in 2014 and 2 SB in 2013). Additionally, he really struggles against left-handed pitchers in his career (.211/.267/.328). Now, the one group of pitchers he does hit is ground-ball pitchers (.302/.349/.475). He is a necessary evil in AL-only leagues, but stay away otherwise because of the minuscule average and speed paired with only okay power. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Don't be fooled by Mike Moustakas's post season heroics. He has no useful fantasy traits besides power and playing time -- and he may even continue to lose playing time against lefties going forward.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Moustakas is generally considered a disappointment after high expectations as a highly touted prospect. While he’s had some ups and downs, we have some idea of what to expect from him now. First, he is going to play everyday for the Royals no matter how he hits -- because of his glove. Also, he will hit between 15 and 25 home runs. He doesn’t have elite power, but an expected known level. The one aspect of his game which is all over the place has been his batting average. It declined every year from his rookie season’s .263 to .212 in 2014. In 2015 it shot up to .284. A few items were at work pulling his average in different directions. The reason for the decline was teams shifted him more, with 2014 being the season he was shifted almost every at bat. His batting average on balls in play sunk to career low .220. His average would have even been worse if he hadn’t been lowering his strikeout rate each season, as well (20% to 16% to 15% to 12%). In 2015, he quit pulling the ball as much into the shift (51% to 39%) and his BABIP shot up to .294. Moose’s key for 2016 is spraying the ball around the field and not pulling the ball into the shift. If you're looking for a breakout, it'll come from showing more power with this new approach. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Moustakas has become an everyday third baseman who will create more value with his glove than his bat. We could see him break out (further) at some point, but don't count on it.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Moustakas didn’t really get a chance to show if his 2015 was a new level (career-best .817 OPS) or just the beginning of a major breakout. He put up a perfectly fine .801 OPS, but in just 113 PA after a collision on a foul ball with Alex Gordon took both players out, Moose for the remainder of the season. The skills we saw in that ’15 season were intact or better and a .214 batting average on balls in play is the only wart on his small sample line of ’16. It is still tough to say for certain, but it looks like the power boost from ’15 was a sign of things to come as he was even better in ’16 with a filthy .260 ISO. If the knee is 100%, Moose looks poised for a big walk year that should yield a mid-20s home run output with a strong batting average (he has struck out less during the power surge) and solid runs scored and driven in totals batting in the heart of Kansas City’s order. (<a href="http://twitter.com/sporer">Paul Sporer</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Moustakas had his season cut short by an ACL injury after just 27 games and now has to show his recent power surge is legitimate in a walk year. The missed time, coupled with depth at third base have pushed his price down beyond pick 200, which mitigates most of the risk. Regularly drafted as a corner infielder, Moose could easily deliver the numbers of a starting third baseman.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

        if (panelo.style.display == 'none') {

        }
        else {
            panelo.style.display = 'none';
            document.getElementById('soa6').style.fontWeight = 'normal';
        }
    }

    function setAverage(t) {
        var elements;
        var browserCheck = (document.all) ? 1 : 0;
        if (t == '*') {
            elements = (ie) ? document.all : document.getElementsByTagName('*');
        } else {
            elements = document.getElementsByTagName(t);
        }
        for (var i = 0; i < elements.length; i++) {
            var node = elements.item(i);
            var vis = elements.item(i).style;
            for (var j = 0; j < node.attributes.length; j++) {
                if (node.attributes.item(j).nodeName == 'class') {
                    if (node.attributes.item(j).nodeValue == 'rgRow grid_average') {


                        if (browserCheck > 0) {

                            if (vis.display == undefined) {
                                vis.dispaly = 'block';
                            }
                            else {
                                if (vis.display == 'block') { vis.display = 'none'; } else { vis.display = 'block'; }
                            }

                        }

                        else {

                            if (vis.display == undefined) {
                                vis.dispaly = 'table-row';
                            }
                            else {
                                if (vis.display == 'table-row') { vis.display = 'none'; } else { vis.display = 'table-row'; }
                            }

                        }

                    }
                }
            }
        }
    }

    function setPart(t) {
        var elements;
        var browserCheck = (document.all) ? 1 : 0;
        if (t == '*') {
            elements = (ie) ? document.all : document.getElementsByTagName('*');
        } else {

        }
        for (var i = 0; i < elements.length; i++) {
            var node = elements.item(i);
            var vis = elements.item(i).style;
            for (var j = 0; j < node.attributes.length; j++) {
                if (node.attributes.item(j).nodeName == 'class') {
                    if (node.attributes.item(j).nodeValue == 'rgRow grid_multi' || node.attributes.item(j).nodeValue == 'rgRow grid_multi') {

    $(document).ready(function () {

        $('a[tipgloss]').each(function () {
            // We make use of the .each() loop to gain access to each element via the "this" keyword...
            $(this).qtip(
		{
		    content: {
		        // Set the text to an image HTML string with the correct src URL to the loading image you want to use
		        text: " ",
		        ajax: {
		            url: $(this).attr('tipgloss') // Use the rel attribute of each element for the url to load
		        }
		    },
		    position: {
		        viewport: $(window),
		        at: "top center",
		        my: "bottom center",
		        effect: false
		    },
		    show: {
		        event: "click mouseenter",
		        solo: true // Only show one tooltip at a time
		    },
		    hide: {
		        event: "click mouseleave",
		        delay: 1000,
		        fixed: true
		    },
		    style: {
		        classes: "qtip-light"
		    }
		})

            // Make sure it doesn't follow the link when we click it
        });
    });
